<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Libertarianism_sidebar" title="Template:Libertarianism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Libertarianism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Libertarianism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Libertarianism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Ronald Ernest</b> "<b>Ron</b>" <b>Paul</b> (born August 20, 1935) is an American author, physician, and former politician. He was formerly the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. Representative</a> for <a href="/wiki/Texas%27s_14th_congressional_district" title="Texas's 14th congressional district">Texas' 14th</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas%27s_22nd_congressional_district" title="Texas's 22nd congressional district">22nd congressional districts</a>. He represented the 22nd congressional district from 1976 to 1977 and from 1979 to 1985, and then represented the 14th congressional district, which included <a href="/wiki/Galveston,_Texas" title="Galveston, Texas">Galveston</a>, from 1997 to 2013. On three occasions, he sought the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">presidency of the United States</a>: as the <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Party_(United_States)" title="Libertarian Party (United States)">Libertarian Party</a> nominee in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1988" title="U.S. presidential election, 1988">1988</a> and as a candidate in the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> primaries of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_2008" title="U.S. presidential election, 2008">2008</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_2012" title="U.S. presidential election, 2012">2012</a>. Paul is a critic of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">federal government</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_policy" title="Fiscal policy">fiscal policies</a>, especially the existence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tax_policy" title="Tax policy">tax policy</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Military%E2%80%93industrial_complex" title="Military–industrial complex">military–industrial complex</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/War_on_Drugs" title="War on Drugs">War on Drugs</a>. Paul has also been a vocal critic of mass surveillance policies such as the <a href="/wiki/Patriot_Act" title="Patriot Act">USA PATRIOT Act</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Global_surveillance_disclosures_(2013%E2%80%93present)" title="Global surveillance disclosures (2013–present)">NSA surveillance programs</a>. Paul was the first chairman of the conservative PAC <a href="/wiki/Citizens_for_a_Sound_Economy" title="Citizens for a Sound Economy">Citizens for a Sound Economy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSE_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSE-2">[2]</a></sup> and has been characterized as the "intellectual godfather" of the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>A native of the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> suburb of <a href="/wiki/Green_Tree,_Pennsylvania" title="Green Tree, Pennsylvania">Green Tree, Pennsylvania</a>, Paul is a graduate of <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg_College" title="Gettysburg College">Gettysburg College</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Duke_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Duke University School of Medicine">Duke University School of Medicine</a>, where he earned his <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">medical degree</a>. He served as a <a href="/wiki/Flight_surgeon" title="Flight surgeon">flight surgeon</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a> from 1963 to 1968. He worked as an <a href="/wiki/Obstetrics_and_gynaecology" title="Obstetrics and gynaecology">obstetrician-gynecologist</a> from the 1960s to the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> He became the first Representative in history to serve concurrently with a son or daughter in the Senate when his son, <a href="/wiki/Rand_Paul" title="Rand Paul">Rand Paul</a>, was elected to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senate</a> from <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul is a Senior Fellow of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mises_Institute" title="Mises Institute">Mises Institute</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and has been an active writer, publishing on the topics of political and economic theory, as well as publicizing the ideas of economists of the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_School" title="Austrian School">Austrian School</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Murray_Rothbard" title="Murray Rothbard">Murray Rothbard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Mises" title="Ludwig von Mises">Ludwig von Mises</a> during his political campaigns. Paul has written <a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul_bibliography" title="Ron Paul bibliography">many books</a> on Austrian economics and <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberal philosophy</a>, beginning with <i>The Case for Gold</i> (1982) and including <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Foreign_Policy_of_Freedom:_Peace,_Commerce,_and_Honest_Friendship" title="A Foreign Policy of Freedom: Peace, Commerce, and Honest Friendship">A Foreign Policy of Freedom</a></i> (2007), <i>Pillars of Prosperity</i> (2008), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Revolution:_A_Manifesto" title="The Revolution: A Manifesto">The Revolution: A Manifesto</a></i> (2008), <i><a href="/wiki/End_the_Fed" title="End the Fed">End the Fed</a></i> (2009) and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberty_Defined:_50_Essential_Issues_That_Affect_Our_Freedom" title="Liberty Defined: 50 Essential Issues That Affect Our Freedom">Liberty Defined</a></i> (2011).</p>
<p>On July 12, 2011, Paul announced that he would forgo seeking another term in Congress in order to focus on <a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul_presidential_campaign,_2012" title="Ron Paul presidential campaign, 2012">his presidential bid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> On May 14, 2012, Paul announced that he would not be competing in any other presidential primaries but that he would still compete for delegates in states where the primary elections have already been held.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washingtontimes05142012_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-washingtontimes05142012-9">[9]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/2012_Republican_National_Convention" title="2012 Republican National Convention">2012 Republican National Convention</a>, Paul received 190 delegate votes. In January 2013, Paul retired from Congress but still remains active on college campuses, giving speeches promoting his libertarian vision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Camia_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Camia-11">[11]</a></sup> Paul received one electoral vote from a <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> <a href="/wiki/Faithless_elector" title="Faithless elector">faithless elector</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">2016 presidential election</a>, making him the oldest person to receive an electoral vote, as well as the second registered <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Party_(United_States)" title="Libertarian Party (United States)">Libertarian</a> Presidential candidate in history to receive an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Electoral_College" title="United States Electoral College">Electoral College</a> vote after <a href="/wiki/John_Hospers" title="John Hospers">John Hospers</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life.2C_education.2C_and_medical_career">Early life, education, and medical career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life, education, and medical career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Ronald Ernest Paul was born on August 20, 1935, in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> the son of Howard Caspar Paul (1904–1997), who ran a small dairy company, and Margaret Paul (née Dumont; 1908–2001). His paternal grandfather emigrated from Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ny_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ny-13">[13]</a></sup> and his paternal grandmother, a devout Christian, was a first-generation German American.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a junior at suburban <a href="/wiki/Keystone_Oaks_High_School" title="Keystone Oaks High School">Dormont High School</a>, he was the <a href="/wiki/200_metres" title="200 metres">200 meter dash</a> state champion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PTR_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-PTR-15">[15]</a></sup> Paul went to <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg_College" title="Gettysburg College">Gettysburg College</a>, where he was also a member of the <a href="/wiki/Lambda_Chi_Alpha" title="Lambda Chi Alpha">Lambda Chi Alpha</a> fraternity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> He graduated with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Science" title="Bachelor of Science">B.S.</a> degree in Biology in 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PTR_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-PTR-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul earned a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">Doctor of Medicine</a> degree from <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Duke_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Duke University School of Medicine">School of Medicine</a> in 1961, and completed his medical <a href="/wiki/Internship_(medicine)" title="Internship (medicine)">internship</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford_Hospital" title="Henry Ford Hospital">Henry Ford Hospital</a> in Detroit and his residency in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obstetrics_and_gynecology" title="Obstetrics and gynecology">obstetrics and gynecology</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magee-Womens_Hospital_of_UPMC" title="Magee-Womens Hospital of UPMC">Magee-Womens Hospital</a> in Pittsburgh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psul_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-psul-18">[18]</a></sup> Paul served as a <a href="/wiki/Flight_surgeon" title="Flight surgeon">flight surgeon</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> from 1963 to 1965 and then in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Air_National_Guard" title="United States Air National Guard">United States Air National Guard</a> from 1965 to 1968. Paul and his wife then relocated to Texas, where he began a private practice in obstetrics and gynecology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psul_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-psul-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_congressional_career_.281976.E2.80.931985.29">Early congressional career (1976–1985)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early congressional career (1976–1985)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While a medical resident in the 1960s, Paul was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Road_to_Serfdom" title="The Road to Serfdom">The Road to Serfdom</a></i>, which caused him to read other publications by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Mises" title="Ludwig von Mises">Ludwig von Mises</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayn_Rand" title="Ayn Rand">Ayn Rand</a>. He came to know economists <a href="/wiki/Hans_Sennholz" title="Hans Sennholz">Hans Sennholz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Murray_Rothbard" title="Murray Rothbard">Murray Rothbard</a> well, and credits to them his interest in the study of economics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gold_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-gold-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>When President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nixon_Shock" title="Nixon Shock">"closed the gold window"</a> by ending American participation in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_System" title="Bretton Woods System">Bretton Woods System</a>, thus ending the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._dollar" title="U.S. dollar">U.S. dollar</a>'s loose association with <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gold_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-gold-19">[19]</a></sup> on August 15, 1971, Paul decided to enter politics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drno_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-drno-20">[20]</a></sup> and became a Republican candidate for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lambda_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-lambda-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections">Elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1974, incumbent <a href="/wiki/Robert_R._Casey" title="Robert R. Casey">Robert R. Casey</a> defeated him for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TX-22" title="TX-22">22nd district</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psul_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-psul-18">[18]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> later appointed Casey to direct the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Maritime_Commission" title="Federal Maritime Commission">Federal Maritime Commission</a>, and Paul won an April 1976 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_election" title="Special election">special election</a> to the vacant office after a <a href="/wiki/Two-round_system" title="Two-round system">runoff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1976-election_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-1976-election-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-casey_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-casey-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Paul lost the next regular election to <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gammage" title="Robert Gammage">Robert Gammage</a> by fewer than 300 votes (0.2%), but defeated Gammage in a 1978 rematch, and was reelected in 1980 and 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fewer-than-300_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-fewer-than-300-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Gammage underestimated Paul's popularity among local mothers: "I had real difficulty down in <a href="/wiki/Brazoria_County,_Texas" title="Brazoria County, Texas">Brazoria County</a>, where he practiced, because he'd delivered half the babies in the county. There were only two obstetricians in the county, and the other one was his partner."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenure">Tenure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Tenure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Paul served in Congress three different periods: first from 1976 to 1977, after he won a special election, then from 1979 to 1985, and finally from 1997 to 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-congress-bio_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-congress-bio-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his early years, Paul served on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_Banking_Committee" title="House Banking Committee">House Banking Committee</a>, where he blamed the Federal Reserve for inflation and spoke against the banking mismanagement that resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan_crisis" title="Savings and loan crisis">savings and loan crisis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ny_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-ny-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008bio_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-2008bio-30">[30]</a></sup> Paul argued for a return to the gold standard maintained by the US from 1873–1933, and with Senator <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Helms" title="Jesse Helms">Jesse Helms</a> convinced the Congress to study the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gold_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-gold-19">[19]</a></sup> He spoke against the reinstatement of registration for the military draft in 1980, in opposition to President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> and the majority of his fellow Republican members of Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj1997_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj1997-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>During his first term, Paul founded the <a href="/wiki/Foundation_for_Rational_Economics_and_Education" title="Foundation for Rational Economics and Education">Foundation for Rational Economics and Education</a> (FREE), a non-profit <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tank</a> dedicated to promoting principles of limited government and free-market economics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPFREE_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPFREE-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPFEE2_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPFEE2-33">[33]</a></sup> In 1984, Paul became the first chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Citizens_for_a_Sound_Economy" title="Citizens for a Sound Economy">Citizens for a Sound Economy</a> (CSE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSE_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-CSE-2">[2]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a> group founded by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Koch" title="Charles Koch">Charles</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Koch" title="David Koch">David Koch</a> "to fight for less government, lower taxes, and less regulation." CSE started a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tea_Party_protest" title="Tea Party protest">Tea Party protest</a> against high taxes in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TEA2002_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-TEA2002-34">[34]</a></sup> In 2004, Citizens for a Sound Economy split into two new organizations, with Citizens for a Sound Economy being renamed as <a href="/wiki/FreedomWorks" title="FreedomWorks">FreedomWorks</a>, and Citizens for a Sound Economy Foundation becoming <a href="/wiki/Americans_for_Prosperity" title="Americans for Prosperity">Americans for Prosperity</a>. The two organizations would become key players in the Tea Party movement from 2009 onward.</p>
<p>Paul proposed <a href="/wiki/Term_limit" title="Term limit">term-limit</a> legislation multiple times, while himself serving four terms in the House of Representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj1997_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-wsj1997-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1984, he decided to retire from the House in order to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_Texas,_1984" title="United States Senate election in Texas, 1984">run for the U.S. Senate</a>, complaining in his House farewell address that "Special interests have replaced the concern that the Founders had for general welfare.... It's difficult for one who loves true liberty and utterly detests the power of the state to come to Washington for a period of time and not leave a true cynic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-insight1997_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-insight1997-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Paul lost the Republican primary to <a href="/wiki/Phil_Gramm" title="Phil Gramm">Phil Gramm</a>, who had switched parties the previous year from Democrat to Republican. Another candidate of the senatorial primary was <a href="/wiki/Henry_Grover" title="Henry Grover">Henry Grover</a>, a conservative former state legislator who had lost the 1972 gubernatorial general election to the Democrat <a href="/wiki/Dolph_Briscoe" title="Dolph Briscoe">Dolph Briscoe</a>, Jr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elections_texas_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-elections_texas-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep-primary_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-rep-primary-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>On Paul's departure from the House, his seat was assumed by former state representative <a href="/wiki/Tom_DeLay" title="Tom DeLay">Tom DeLay</a>, who would later become <a href="/wiki/Party_leaders_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Party leaders of the United States House of Representatives">House Majority Leader</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Libertarian_Party_and_ventures">Libertarian Party and ventures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Libertarian Party and ventures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1985.E2.80.931997">1985–1997</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1985–1997">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the loss of the 1984 senate race, Paul returned to his obstetrics practice and took part in a number of other business ventures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ny_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-ny-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Along with his former congressional chief of staff, <a href="/wiki/Lew_Rockwell" title="Lew Rockwell">Lew Rockwell</a>, Paul founded a for-profit enterprise, Ron Paul &amp; Associates, Inc. (RP&amp;A) in 1984, with Paul serving as president, Rockwell as vice president, Paul's wife Carol as secretary, and daughter Lori Pyeatt as treasurer. The company published a variety of <a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul_newsletters" title="Ron Paul newsletters">political and investment-oriented newsletters</a>, including <i>Ron Paul Freedom Report</i> and <i>Ron Paul Survival Report</i>, and by 1993 was generating revenues in excess of $900,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JKAWM_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-JKAWM-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1995_video_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-1995_video-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul also co-owned a mail-order coin dealership, <i>Ron Paul Coins</i>, for twelve years with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burt_Blumert" title="Burt Blumert">Burt Blumert</a>, who continued to operate the dealership after Paul resumed office in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cw_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-cw-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Paul spoke multiple times at the <a href="/wiki/American_Numismatic_Association" title="American Numismatic Association">American Numismatic Association</a>'s 1988 convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cw_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-cw-43">[43]</a></sup> He worked with his Foundation for Rational Economics and Education on such projects as establishing the National Endowment for Liberty, producing the <i>At Issue</i> public policy series that was broadcast on the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Channel" title="Discovery Channel">Discovery Channel</a> and <a href="/wiki/CNBC" title="CNBC">CNBC</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPFREE_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-RPFREE-32">[32]</a></sup> and continuing publication of newsletters.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1988_presidential_campaign">1988 presidential campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1988 presidential campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul_presidential_campaign,_1988" title="Ron Paul presidential campaign, 1988">Ron Paul presidential campaign, 1988</a></div>
<p>Paul resigned from the Republican Party in 1987 and launched a bid for the presidency running on the <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Party_(United_States)" title="Libertarian Party (United States)">Libertarian Party</a> ticket. His candidacy was seen as problematic because of the party's long support for freedom of choice on abortions. <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> activist <a href="/wiki/Russell_Means" title="Russell Means">Russell Means</a> emphasized that he was pro-choice on the abortion issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In a forum held prior to the nomination, Means dismissed the greater funds raised by Paul's campaign, commenting that Means was receiving "10 times more press" than the former Congressman and was therefore "100 times more effective".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1988 presidential election, Paul was on the ballot in 46 states,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1988nytimes_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-1988nytimes-47">[47]</a></sup> scoring third in the popular vote with 432,179 votes (0.5%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Paul was kept off the ballot in Missouri, due to what the <i><a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Post-Dispatch" title="St. Louis Post-Dispatch">St. Louis Post-Dispatch</a></i> termed a "technicality," and received votes there only when written in,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dispatch1_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dispatch1-49">[49]</a></sup> just as he did in North Carolina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Paul, his presidential campaign was about more than obtaining office; he sought to promote his libertarian ideas, often to school and university groups regardless of vote eligibility. He said, "We're just as interested in the future generation as this election. These kids will vote eventually, and maybe, just maybe, they'll go home and talk to their parents."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1988nytimes_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-1988nytimes-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul considered campaigning for President in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1992" title="United States presidential election, 1992">1992</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> but instead chose to endorse <a href="/wiki/Pat_Buchanan" title="Pat Buchanan">Pat Buchanan</a> that year, and served as an adviser to Buchanan's Republican presidential primary campaign against incumbent President George H. W. Bush.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_congressional_career_.281997.E2.80.932013.29">Later congressional career (1997–2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Later congressional career (1997–2013)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ron_paul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1375" data-file-width="866" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ron_paul.jpg/170px-Ron_paul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ron_paul.jpg/255px-Ron_paul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ron_paul.jpg/340px-Ron_paul.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ron_paul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An earlier congressional portrait of Paul</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections_2">Elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Texas%27s_14th_congressional_district" title="Texas's 14th congressional district">Texas's 14th congressional district</a>.</div>
<dl>
<dt>1996 campaign</dt>
</dl>
<p>During 1996, Paul was re-elected to Congress after a difficult campaign. The <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> endorsed incumbent <a href="/wiki/Greg_Laughlin" title="Greg Laughlin">Greg Laughlin</a> in the primary; Paul won with assistance from baseball pitcher, constituent, and friend <a href="/wiki/Nolan_Ryan" title="Nolan Ryan">Nolan Ryan</a>, tax activist and publisher <a href="/wiki/Steve_Forbes" title="Steve Forbes">Steve Forbes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ny_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-ny-13">[13]</a></sup> and conservative commentator <a href="/wiki/Pat_Buchanan" title="Pat Buchanan">Pat Buchanan</a> (the latter two of whom had had presidential campaigns that year). Paul narrowly defeated Democratic attorney Charles "Lefty" Morris in the fall election, despite Morris' criticism over controversial statements in several <a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul_newsletters" title="Ron Paul newsletters">newsletters that Paul published</a>.</p>
<dl>
<dt>1998–2013</dt>
</dl>
<p>In 1998 and 2000, Paul defeated Loy Sneary, a Democratic <a href="/wiki/Bay_City,_Texas" title="Bay City, Texas">Bay City, Texas</a>, rice farmer and former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matagorda_County" title="Matagorda County">Matagorda County</a> judge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drno_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-drno-20">[20]</a></sup> In the 2008 Republican primary,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> he defeated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friendswood" title="Friendswood">Friendswood</a> city councilman <a href="/wiki/Chris_Peden" title="Chris Peden">Chris Peden</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> with over 70 percent of the vote<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and ran unopposed in the general election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In the 2010 Republican primary, Paul defeated three opponents with 80 percent of the vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 12, 2011, Paul announced that he would not seek re-election to the House in order to pursue the 2012 presidential election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-free_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-free-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenure_2">Tenure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tenure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl>
<dt>Legislation</dt>
</dl>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_legislation_sponsored_by_Ron_Paul" title="List of legislation sponsored by Ron Paul">List of legislation sponsored by Ron Paul</a></div>
<p>Of the 620 bills that Paul had sponsored through December 2011, over a period of more than 22 years in Congress, only one had been signed into law – a lifetime success rate of less than 0.3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPHR2121_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPHR2121-60">[60]</a></sup> The sole measure authored by Paul that was ultimately enacted allowed for a federal customhouse to be sold to a local historic preservation society (H.R. 2121 in 2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPHR2121_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-WPHR2121-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>By amending other legislation, he has helped prohibit funding for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_identification_numbers" title="National identification numbers">national identification numbers</a>, funding for federal teacher certification,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drno_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-drno-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a> jurisdiction over the U.S. military, American participation with any U.N. <a href="/wiki/Tobin_tax" title="Tobin tax">global tax</a>, and surveillance of peaceful <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a> activities by citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Affiliations</dt>
</dl>
<p>Paul was honorary chairman of, and is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Liberty_Caucus" title="Republican Liberty Caucus">Republican Liberty Caucus</a>, a political action committee that describes its goal as electing "liberty-minded, limited-government individuals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> He is an initiating member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congressional_Rural_Caucus" title="Congressional Rural Caucus">Congressional Rural Caucus</a>, which deals with agricultural and rural issues, and the 140-member <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Wildlife_Refuge_Caucus" title="Congressional Wildlife Refuge Caucus">Congressional Wildlife Refuge Caucus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Committee_assignments">Committee assignments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Committee assignments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Paul served on the following committee and subcommittees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Financial_Services" title="United States House Committee on Financial Services">Committee on Financial Services</a></b>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Financial_Services_Subcommittee_on_Domestic_Monetary_Policy_and_Technology" title="United States House Financial Services Subcommittee on Domestic Monetary Policy and Technology">Subcommittee on Domestic Monetary Policy and Technology</a> (Chairman)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_House_Financial_Services_Subcommittee_on_International_Monetary_Policy_and_Trade" title="United States House Financial Services Subcommittee on International Monetary Policy and Trade">Subcommittee on International Monetary Policy and Trade</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Foreign_Affairs" title="United States House Committee on Foreign Affairs">Committee on Foreign Affairs</a></b>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Foreign_Affairs_Subcommittee_on_Oversight_and_Investigations" title="United States House Foreign Affairs Subcommittee on Oversight and Investigations">Subcommittee on Oversight and Investigations</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>With the election of the 112th Congress, and a resulting GOP majority in the House, Paul became the chairman of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Financial_Services_Subcommittee_on_Domestic_Monetary_Policy_and_Technology" title="United States House Financial Services Subcommittee on Domestic Monetary Policy and Technology">Subcommittee on Domestic Monetary Policy and Technology</a> starting in January 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ronpaul01216_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ronpaul01216-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul's congressional career ended on January 3, 2013 with the swearing in of the 113th Congress.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2008_presidential_campaign">2008 presidential campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 2008 presidential campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ronpaul5.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="449" data-file-width="480" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ronpaul5.JPG/220px-Ronpaul5.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ronpaul5.JPG/330px-Ronpaul5.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ronpaul5.JPG/440px-Ronpaul5.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ronpaul5.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Paul being interviewed the day of the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_primary" title="New Hampshire primary">New Hampshire primary</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manchester,_New_Hampshire" title="Manchester, New Hampshire">Manchester</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul_presidential_campaign,_2008" title="Ron Paul presidential campaign, 2008">Ron Paul presidential campaign, 2008</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2008" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 2008">Republican Party presidential primaries, 2008</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2008_Republican_primary_campaign">2008 Republican primary campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 2008 Republican primary campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Paul formally declared his candidacy for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008_Republican_nomination" title="2008 Republican nomination">2008 Republican nomination</a> on March 12, 2007, on <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Few major politicians endorsed him, and his campaign was largely ignored by traditional media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> However, he attracted an intensely loyal grassroots following, in large part energized by "iconoclastic white men"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTSeelye07_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTSeelye07-68">[68]</a></sup> interacting through internet social media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPdigg_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPdigg-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In May 2007, shortly after the first televised primary debates, the blogs search engine site <a href="/wiki/Technorati" title="Technorati">Technorati</a>.com listed Paul's name as the term most frequently searched for;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPdigg_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-RPdigg-69">[69]</a></sup> and Paul's campaign claimed that Paul had more <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> channel subscribers than <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> or any other candidate for president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> For a candidate who had had relatively low national name recognition prior to entering the race, Paul did surprisingly well in fundraising, taking in more money than any other Republican candidate in the fourth quarter of 2007, as the primary season headed into the Iowa caucuses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite benefiting from large numbers of campaign contributions from individual donors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and the efforts of tech-savvy supporters determined to keep his name a frequent topic of discussion on the internet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPdigg_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-RPdigg-69">[69]</a></sup> over the course of the campaign Paul was unable to translate the enthusiasm of his core supporters into large enough numbers of actual primary votes to unseat his rivals.</p>
<p>Paul came in 5th place in both the January 4 <a href="/wiki/Iowa_caucuses" title="Iowa caucuses">Iowa caucuses</a> (10% of votes cast)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and the January 8 New Hampshire primary (8%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> With the exception of the Nevada caucuses January 19, where he came in 2nd (14%) behind Romney (51%), he did little better through the rest of January: Michigan 4th (6%), South Carolina 5th (4%), Florida 5th (3%). On SuperTuesday, February 5, he placed 4th in almost every state, generally taking in a mere 3–6% of the votes although he did better in the northern states of North Dakota (21%, 3rd place) and Montana (25%, 2nd place).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>By March, front-runner <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a> had secured enough pledged delegates to guarantee that he would win the nomination, and Romney and Huckabee had both formally withdrawn from the race. Paul, who had won no state primaries, knew that it was now mathematically impossible for him to win the nomination, as he had captured only 20<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> – 40 pledged delegates compared to more than 1,191 for McCain, yet he refused to concede the race and said that it was unlikely that he would ultimately endorse McCain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElkinsBeast_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-ElkinsBeast-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Over the next few weeks, Paul's supporters clashed with establishment Republicans at several county and state party conventions over state party rules, the party platforms, and selection of delegates for the national convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In one of the more dramatic moments, Nevada's state party leaders, outmaneuvered by Paul supporters at the state nominating convention, resorted to the highly unusual measure of prematurely and abruptly shutting down the convention before selecting national delegates, with a plan to reconvene at a later date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 12, 2008, Paul finally withdrew his bid for the Republican nomination. He later said that one of the reasons he did not run in the general election as a third-party candidate, after losing the primaries, was that, as a concession to gain ballot access in certain states, he had signed legally binding agreements to not run a third-party campaign if he lost the primary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Some of the $4 million remaining campaign contributions was invested into the new political action and advocacy group called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ron_Paul%27s_Campaign_for_Liberty" title="Ron Paul's Campaign for Liberty">Ron Paul's Campaign for Liberty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refusal_to_endorse_the_Republican_nominee">Refusal to endorse the Republican nominee</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Refusal to endorse the Republican nominee">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At a September 10, 2008, press conference, Paul announced his general support of four third-party candidates: <a href="/wiki/Cynthia_McKinney" title="Cynthia McKinney">Cynthia McKinney</a> (<a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_the_United_States" title="Green Party of the United States">Green Party</a>); <a href="/wiki/Bob_Barr" title="Bob Barr">Bob Barr</a> (<a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Party_(United_States)" title="Libertarian Party (United States)">Libertarian Party</a>); <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Baldwin" title="Chuck Baldwin">Chuck Baldwin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Constitution_Party_(United_States)" title="Constitution Party (United States)">Constitution Party</a>); and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a> (independent). He said that each of them had pledged to adhere to a policy of balancing budgets, bringing the troops home, defending privacy and personal liberties, and investigating the Federal Reserve. Paul also said that under no circumstances would he be endorsing either of the two main parties' candidates (McCain – Republican Party, or Obama – Democratic Party) because there were no real differences between them, and because neither of them, if elected, would seek to make the fundamental changes in governance that were necessary. He urged instead that, rather than contribute to the “charade” that the two-party election system had become, the voters support the third-party candidates as a protest vote, to force change in the election process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kugel_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-kugel-92">[92]</a></sup> Later that same day, Paul gave a televised interview with Nader saying much the same again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two weeks later, "shocked and disappointed" that <a href="/wiki/Bob_Barr" title="Bob Barr">Bob Barr</a> (the Libertarian nominee) had pulled out of attending the press conference at the last minute and had admonished Paul for remaining neutral and failing to say which specific candidate Paul would vote for in the general election, Paul released a statement saying that he had decided to endorse <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Baldwin" title="Chuck Baldwin">Chuck Baldwin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Party_(United_States)" title="Constitution Party (United States)">Constitution Party</a> candidate, for president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campaignforliberty1_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-campaignforliberty1-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul withdrew from active campaigning in the last weeks of the primary election period. He received 42,426 votes, or 0.03% of the total cast, in the general election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_presidential_campaign">2012 presidential campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 2012 presidential campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul_presidential_campaign,_2012" title="Ron Paul presidential campaign, 2012">Ron Paul presidential campaign, 2012</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Draft_Ron_Paul_movement" title="Draft Ron Paul movement">Draft Ron Paul movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2012" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 2012">Republican Party presidential primaries, 2012</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_Republican_primary_campaign">2012 Republican primary campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 2012 Republican primary campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Paul won several early straw polls for the 2012 Republican presidential nomination<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> and in late April 2011, he formed an official exploratory committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> He participated in the first Republican presidential debate on May 5, 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-election-news_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-election-news-99">[99]</a></sup> and on May 13, 2011, Paul formally announced his candidacy in an interview on <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Morning_America" title="Good Morning America">Good Morning America</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> He placed second in the 2011 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ames_Straw_Poll" title="Ames Straw Poll">Ames Straw Poll</a>, missing first by 0.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2011, with Paul's increased support, the controversy over racist and homophobic statements in several Ron Paul newsletters in the 1980s and early 1990s once again gained media attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> During this time Paul supporters asserted that he was continually ignored by the media despite his significant support, citing examples of where television news shows would fail to mention Paul in discussions of the Republican presidential hopefuls even when he was polling second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ron Paul’s presidential campaign paid former Iowa state senator <a href="/wiki/Kent_Sorenson" title="Kent Sorenson">Kent Sorenson</a> $73,000 to switch his support from Rep. <a href="/wiki/Michele_Bachmann" title="Michele Bachmann">Michele Bachmann</a> to Paul. In court papers filed in August 2014, Sorenson said that he had been paid by both presidential campaigns for his endorsement and plead guilty to criminal charges stemming from the incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul came in third in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iowa_Republican_caucuses,_2012" title="Iowa Republican caucuses, 2012">Iowa Republican Caucus</a> held on January 3, 2012. Out of a turnout of 121,503 votes, Paul took 26,036 (21%) of the certified votes. <a href="/wiki/Rick_Santorum" title="Rick Santorum">Rick Santorum</a> and Mitt Romney finished in a virtual tie for first place with 25% each,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> although Ron Paul had ultimately <a class="external text" href="http://elections.nytimes.com/2012/primaries/states/iowa?ref=politics" rel="nofollow">won</a> Iowa at the Republican National Convention gathering 22 delegates to Mitt Romney's 5. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Republican_primary,_2012" title="New Hampshire Republican primary, 2012">New Hampshire Primary</a> held on January 10, 2012, Paul received 23% of the votes and came in second after Romney's 39%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul's results then declined, despite the withdrawal of candidates Michele Bachmann, Jon Huntsman and Rick Perry. He had fourth-place finishes in the next two primaries, on January 21 in South Carolina (with 13% of the vote<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>) and on January 31 in Florida (where he received 7% of the vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FLEW_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FLEW-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vote_by_COUNTY_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vote_by_COUNTY-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>)</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ron_Paul_0723.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2508" data-file-width="2358" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ron_Paul_0723.jpg/220px-Ron_Paul_0723.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ron_Paul_0723.jpg/330px-Ron_Paul_0723.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ron_Paul_0723.jpg/440px-Ron_Paul_0723.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ron_Paul_0723.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Paul speaking at a rally <a href="/wiki/Lindenwood_University" title="Lindenwood University">Lindenwood University</a> in <a href="/wiki/St._Charles,_Missouri" title="St. Charles, Missouri">St. Charles, Missouri</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On February 4, Paul finished third in Nevada with 18.8% of the vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Three non-binding primaries were held on February 7; Paul took 3rd place in Colorado<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CO2012_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-CO2012-115">[115]</a></sup> and Missouri<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MO2012_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-MO2012-116">[116]</a></sup> with 13% and 12% of the vote respectively. He fared better in Minnesota<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MN2012_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-MN2012-117">[117]</a></sup> with 27%, finishing second to Rick Santorum.</p>
<p>On May 14, Paul's campaign announced that due to lack of funds (though despite financial backing from financiers <a href="/wiki/Peter_Thiel" title="Peter Thiel">Peter Thiel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Spitznagel" title="Mark Spitznagel">Mark Spitznagel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>) he would no longer actively campaign for votes in the 11 remaining primary states, including Texas and California, that had not yet voted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washingtontimes05142012_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-washingtontimes05142012-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC0514_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABC0514-119">[119]</a></sup> He would, however, continue to seek to win delegates for the national party convention in the states that had already voted.</p>
<p>In June, a group of 132 supporters of Paul, demanding the freedom as delegates to the upcoming Republican party national convention to cast votes for Paul, filed a lawsuit in U.S. District Court against the <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> and 55 state and territorial Republican party organizations for allegedly coercing delegates to choose <a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney" title="Mitt Romney">Mitt Romney</a> as the party’s presidential nominee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NJsuit_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-NJsuit-120">[120]</a></sup> The suit alleged that there had been “a systematic campaign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Election_fraud" title="Election fraud">election fraud</a> at state conventions,” employing rigging of voting machines, ballot stuffing, and falsification of ballot totals. The suit further pointed to incidents at state conventions, including acts of violence and changes in procedural rules, allegedly intended to deny participation of Paul supporters in the party decision-making and to prevent votes from being cast for Paul. An attorney representing the complainants said that Paul campaign advisor <a href="/wiki/Doug_Wead" title="Doug Wead">Doug Wead</a> had voiced support for the legal action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NJsuit_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-NJsuit-120">[120]</a></sup> Paul himself told <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a> that although the lawsuit was not a part of his campaign’s strategy and that he had not been advising his supporters to sue, he was not going to tell his supporters not to sue, if they had a legitimate argument. “If they’re not following the rules, you have a right to stand up for the rules. I think for the most part these winning caucuses that we've been involved in we have followed the rules. And the other side has at times not followed the rules.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNNsuit_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNNsuit-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_National_convention">Republican National convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Republican National convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Paul declined to speak at the Republican National Convention as a matter of principle, saying that the convention planners had demanded that his remarks be vetted by the Romney campaign and that he make an unqualified endorsement of Romney.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytspeech_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytspeech-122">[122]</a></sup> Paul had felt that "It wouldn’t be my speech.... That would undo everything I’ve done in the last 30 years. I don’t fully endorse him for president.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytspeech_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytspeech-122">[122]</a></sup> Many of Paul's supporters and delegates walked out of the convention in protest over rules adopted by the convention that reduced their delegate count and that would make it harder for non-establishment candidates to win the party's nomination in future elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Supporters and media commentators had noted that the delegations from states where Paul had had the most support were given the worst seats in the convention hall, while delegations from regions with no electoral votes, such as the Northern Mariana Islands, American Samoa, and Puerto Rico, were given prime seats at the front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refusal_to_endorse_the_Republican_nominee_2">Refusal to endorse the Republican nominee</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Refusal to endorse the Republican nominee">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As in 2008, in 2012 Paul ultimately refused to endorse the ticket selected by the Republican Party. He said that there was no essential difference between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romney%E2%80%93Ryan" title="Romney–Ryan">Romney</a> and his Democratic opponent, President Obama, on the most critical policies: "I've been in this business a long time and believe me there is essentially no difference from one administration to another no matter what the platforms.... The foreign policy stays the same, the monetary policy stays the same, there’s no proposal for any real cuts and both parties support it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul received 26,204 write-in votes, or 0.02% of the total cast in the election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_party_identification">Political party identification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Political party identification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Throughout his entire tenure in Congress, Paul has represented his district as a member of the Republican Party. However, he has frequently taken positions in direct opposition to the other members and the leadership of the party, and he has sometimes publicly questioned whether he really belonged in the party.</p>
<p>Paul voted for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> for president in 1956 when he was 21 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DearFrank_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-DearFrank-128">[128]</a></sup> He had been a lifelong supporter of the Republican Party by the time he entered politics in the mid-1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DearFrank_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-DearFrank-128">[128]</a></sup> He was one of the first elected officials in the nation to support <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>'s presidential campaign,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFC88_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFC88-129">[129]</a></sup> and he actively campaigned for Reagan in 1976 and 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DMN87_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-DMN87-130">[130]</a></sup> After Reagan's election in 1980, Paul quickly became disillusioned with the Reagan administration's policies. He later recalled being the only Republican to vote against Reagan budget proposals in 1981,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SJMN88_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-SJMN88-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT88_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-LAT88-132">[132]</a></sup> aghast that "in 1977, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> proposed a budget with a $38 billion deficit, and every Republican in the House voted against it. In 1981, Reagan proposed a budget with a $45 billion deficit – which turned out to be $113 billion – and Republicans were cheering his great victory. They were living in a storybook land."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFC88_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-SFC88-129">[129]</a></sup> He expressed his disgust with the political culture of both major parties in a speech delivered in 1984 upon resigning from the House of Representatives to prepare for a (failed) run for the Senate, and he eventually apologized to his libertarian friends for having supported Reagan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SJMN88_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-SJMN88-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikisource-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="38"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Ron_Paul%27s_1987_Resignation_Letter_to_the_RNC" title="wikisource:Ron Paul's 1987 Resignation Letter to the RNC">Ron Paul's 1987 Resignation Letter to the RNC</a></b></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>By 1987, Paul was ready to sever all ties to the Republican Party, as he explained in a blistering resignation letter: "Since [1981] Ronald Reagan and the Republican Party have given us skyrocketing deficits, and astoundingly a doubled national debt. How is it that the party of balanced budgets, with control of the White House and Senate, accumulated red ink greater than all previous administrations put together? … There is no credibility left for the Republican Party as a force to reduce the size of government. That is the message of the Reagan years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DearFrank_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-DearFrank-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DMN87_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-DMN87-130">[130]</a></sup> A month later he announced he would seek the 1988 Libertarian Party nomination for president.</p>
<p>During the 1988 campaign, Paul called Reagan "a dramatic failure"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DMN87_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-DMN87-130">[130]</a></sup> and complained that "Reagan's record is disgraceful. He starts wars, breaks the law, supplies terrorists with guns made at taxpayers' expense and lies about it to the American people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PI87_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-PI87-133">[133]</a></sup> Paul predicted that "the Republicans are on their way out as a major party,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SJMN88_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-SJMN88-131">[131]</a></sup> and he said that, although registered as a Republican, he had always been a libertarian at heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SJMN88_131-3"><a href="#cite_note-SJMN88-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT88_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-LAT88-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul returned to his private medical practice and managing several business ventures after losing the 1988 election; but by 1996, he was ready to return to politics, this time running on the Republican Party ticket again. He said that he had never read the entire Libertarian platform when he ran for president as a Libertarian in 1988, and that "I worked for the Libertarians on my terms, not theirs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chron96_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-chron96-134">[134]</a></sup> He added that in terms of a political label he preferred to call himself "a constitutionalist. In Congress I took an oath to uphold the Constitution, not the (Republican) platform."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chron96_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-chron96-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>When he lost the Republican Party presidential primary election in 2008, Paul criticized the two major political parties, saying that there was no real difference between the parties and that neither of them truly intended to challenge the status quo. He refused to endorse the Republican Party's nominee for president, <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a>, and lent his support to third-party candidates instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 2012 presidential campaign, during which he acknowledged it was unlikely that he would win the Republican Party nomination,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Paul again asserted that he was participating in the Republican Party on his own terms, trying to persuade the rest of the party to move toward his positions rather than joining in with theirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> He expressed doubt that he would support any of his rivals should they win the nomination, warning that, “If the policies of the Republican Party are the same as the Democrat Party and they don't want to change anything on foreign policy, they don't want to cut anything, they don't want to audit the Fed and find out about monetary policy, they don't want to have actual change in government, that is a problem for me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> On that same theme he said in another interview, "I would be reluctant to jump on board and tell all of the supporters that have given me trust and money that all of a sudden, I'd say, [all] we've done is for naught. So, let's support anybody at all … even if they disagree with everything that we do."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_positions">Political positions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Political positions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_positions_of_Ron_Paul" title="Political positions of Ron Paul">Political positions of Ron Paul</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ronpaul1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="780" data-file-width="1024" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Ronpaul1.jpg/220px-Ronpaul1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Ronpaul1.jpg/330px-Ronpaul1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Ronpaul1.jpg/440px-Ronpaul1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ronpaul1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Paul at the 2007 <a href="/wiki/National_Right_to_Life_Committee" title="National Right to Life Committee">National Right to Life Committee</a> Convention in <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City,_Missouri" title="Kansas City, Missouri">Kansas City, Missouri</a>, June 15, 2007.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Paul has been described as <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_in_the_United_States" title="Libertarianism in the United States">libertarian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ny_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-ny-13">[13]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia" title="University of Georgia">University of Georgia</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_scientist" title="Political scientist">political scientist</a> Keith Poole, Paul had the most conservative voting record of any member of Congress from 1937 to 2002,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> and is the most conservative of the candidates that had sought the 2012 Republican nomination for president,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> on a scale primarily measuring positions on the role of government in managing the economy – not positions on social issues or foreign policy matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Other analyses, in which key votes on domestic social issues and foreign policy factor more heavily, have judged Paul much more moderate. The <i><a href="/wiki/National_Journal" title="National Journal">National Journal</a></i>, for instance, rated Paul only the 145th most conservative member of the House of Representatives (out of 435) based on votes cast in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>The foundation of Paul's political philosophy is the conviction that "the proper role for government in America is to provide national defense, a court system for civil disputes, a criminal justice system for acts of force and fraud, and little else."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> He has been nicknamed "Dr. No,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drno_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-drno-20">[20]</a></sup> reflecting both his medical degree and his insistence that he will "never vote for legislation unless the proposed measure is expressly authorized by the Constitution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008bio_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-2008bio-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defense">Defense</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Defense">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Paul's foreign policy of nonintervention<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> made him the only 2008 Republican presidential candidate to have voted against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iraq_War_Resolution" title="Iraq War Resolution">Iraq War Resolution</a> in 2002. He advocates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Sovereignty_Restoration_Act" title="American Sovereignty Restoration Act">withdrawal</a> from the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, and from the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a>, for reasons of maintaining strong national sovereignty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>He voted for the <a href="/wiki/Authorization_for_Use_of_Military_Force_Against_Terrorists" title="Authorization for Use of Military Force Against Terrorists">Authorization for Use of Military Force Against Terrorists</a> in response to the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>, but suggested war alternatives such as authorizing the president to grant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Letters_of_Marque_and_Reprisal" title="Letters of Marque and Reprisal">Letters of Marque and Reprisal</a> targeting specific terrorists. An opponent of the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a> and potential war with Iran, he has also criticized <a href="/wiki/Neoconservatism" title="Neoconservatism">neoconservatism</a> and U.S. foreign policy in the Middle East, arguing that both inadvertently cause terrorist reprisals against Americans, such as the 9/11 attacks. Paul has stated that "Israel is our close friend" and that it is not the place of the United States to "dictate how Israel runs her affairs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-israel_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-israel-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic">Domestic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Domestic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Paul endorses <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_right" title="Constitutional right">constitutional rights</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_keep_and_bear_arms" title="Right to keep and bear arms">right to keep and bear arms</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a></i> for political detainees. He opposes the <a href="/wiki/Patriot_Act" title="Patriot Act">Patriot Act</a>, federal use of <a href="/wiki/Torture_and_the_United_States" title="Torture and the United States">torture</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presidential_autonomy" title="Presidential autonomy">presidential autonomy</a>, a <a href="/wiki/REAL_ID_Act" title="REAL ID Act">national identification card</a>, warrantless <a href="/wiki/NSA_warrantless_surveillance_(2001%E2%80%9307)" title="NSA warrantless surveillance (2001–07)">domestic surveillance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_States" title="Conscription in the United States">the draft</a>. Paul also believes that the notion of the <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a> is currently misused by the court system: "In case after case, the Supreme Court has used the infamous 'separation of church and state' metaphor to uphold court decisions that allow the federal government to intrude upon and deprive citizens of their religious liberty."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sometime within the same month but much after the event of authorities executing a lock-down in sequence to the April 2013 <a href="/wiki/Boston_Marathon_bombing" title="Boston Marathon bombing">Boston Marathon bombing</a>, Paul commented on the tactics used by governing forces into a harsh criticism that he has written as a "military-style occupation of an American city".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Camia_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Camia-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic">Economic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ron_Paul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Economic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
